        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.1c12171"><meta property="og:title" content="Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/acsami.1c12171.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Developing hard carbon with a high initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) and very good cycling stability is of great importance for practical sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Defects and oxygen-containing groups grown along either the carbon edges or the layers, however, are inevitable in hard carbon and can cause a tremendous density of irreversible Na+ sites, decreasing the efficiency and therefore causing failure of the battery. Thus, eliminating these unexpected defect structures is significant for enhancing the battery performance. Herein, we develop a strategy of applying a soft-carbon coating onto free-standing hard-carbon electrodes, which greatly hinders the formation of defects and oxygen-containing groups on hard carbon. The electrochemical results show that the soft-carbon-coated, free-standing hard-carbon electrodes can achieve an ultrahigh ICE of 94.1% and long cycling performance (99% capacity retention after 100 cycles at a current density of 20 mA g–1), demonstrating their great potential in practical sodium storage systems. The sodium storage mechanism was also investigated by operando Raman spectroscopy. Our sodium storage mechanism extends the “adsorption–intercalation–pore filling–deposition” model. We propose that the pore filling in the plateau area might be divided into two parts: (1) sodium could fill in the pores near the inner wall of the carbon layer; (2) when the sodium in the inner wall pores is close to saturation, the sodium could be further deposited onto the existing sodium."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/acsami.1c12171.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e41b4cb2775c-LHR"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e41b4cb2775c-LHR">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '05f03f13-4fb0-4126-9b46-65e343442ddb'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e41b4cb2775c-LHR">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90f6e41b4cb2775c-LHR">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c12171", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c12171", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c12171", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c12171", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c12171", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c12171", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c12171", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c12171", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Soft-Carbon-Coated, Free-Standing, Low-Defect, Hard-Carbon Anode To Achieve a 94% Initial Coulombic Efficiency for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c12171", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

R. class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Minghong++Wu" data-id="article_author_search">Minghong Wu</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D44358%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DXiang-Xi%2BHe%252C%2BJia-Hua%2BZhao%252C%2BWei-Hong%2BLai%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D37%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c12171%26title%3DSoft-Carbon-Coated%252C%2BFree-Standing%252C%2BLow-Defect%252C%2BHard-Carbon%2BAnode%2BTo%2BAchieve%2Ba%2B94%2525%2BInitial%2BCoulombic%2BEfficiency%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D44368%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2021" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0010.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Developing hard carbon with a high initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) and very good cycling stability is of great importance for practical sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Defects and oxygen-containing groups grown along either the carbon edges or the layers, however, are inevitable in hard carbon and can cause a tremendous density of irreversible Na<sup>+</sup> sites, decreasing the efficiency and therefore causing failure of the battery. Thus, eliminating these unexpected defect structures is significant for enhancing the battery performance. Herein, we develop a strategy of applying a soft-carbon coating onto free-standing hard-carbon electrodes, which greatly hinders the formation of defects and oxygen-containing groups on hard carbon. The electrochemical results show that the soft-carbon-coated, free-standing hard-carbon electrodes can achieve an ultrahigh ICE of 94.1% and long cycling performance (99% capacity retention after 100 cycles at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), demonstrating their great potential in practical sodium storage systems. The sodium storage mechanism was also investigated by operando Raman spectroscopy. Our sodium storage mechanism extends the “adsorption–intercalation–pore filling–deposition” model. We propose that the pore filling in the plateau area might be divided into two parts: (1) sodium could fill in the pores near the inner wall of the carbon layer; (2) when the sodium in the inner wall pores is close to saturation, the sodium could be further deposited onto the existing sodium.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D44358%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DXiang-Xi%2BHe%252C%2BJia-Hua%2BZhao%252C%2BWei-Hong%2BLai%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D37%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c12171%26title%3DSoft-Carbon-Coated%252C%2BFree-Standing%252C%2BLow-Defect%252C%2BHard-Carbon%2BAnode%2BTo%2BAchieve%2Ba%2B94%2525%2BInitial%2BCoulombic%2BEfficiency%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D44368%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Anode%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon" class="article__tags__link">Carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291356&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Defects" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Defects" class="article__tags__link">Defects</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Layers" class="article__tags__link">Layers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hard+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hard%20carbon">hard carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Soft+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_soft%20carbon">soft carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Spatial+Coating&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_spatial%20coating">spatial coating</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Storage+Mechanism&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20storage%20mechanism">sodium storage mechanism</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as a strong competitor in the market for commercial batteries and are expanding their applications on various devices to replace lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in the future owing to the high sustainability and economic efficiency of sodium. Competitive electrode materials in SIBs must have stable and safe electrochemical performance, with the expectations of being cost effective and environmentally friendly. Over the years, a variety of anodes have been developed for SIBs. The typical conversion-type <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> and alloy-type <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 3 to 8">(3−8)</a> anode materials, however, are facing challenges, such as high cost, low energy density, and volume expansion. Hard carbon can achieve a high specific capacity at a very low potential plateau, offering the benefit of raising the energy density of the full batteries. In addition to its low cost and the sustainability of Na sources, hard carbon therefore has been considered as the most promising and appropriate anode material for sodium-ion batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Nevertheless, hard carbon suffers from low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) and poor cycling performance, which hinders its practical application. The possible reason for low ICE is that Na<sup>+</sup> is irreversibly captured by defects/functional groups in hard carbon and the solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer. Early studies attributed the low ICE to its large specific surface area, which can lead to side reactions with electrolytes. The debates on the electrochemical mechanism of hard carbon, however, have indicated that the hypothesis of effects arising from the specific surface area is still controversial, as some reports in the literature have shown that hard carbon with a low specific surface area can also lead to a poor ICE. Defects created during the thermal process have been considered as another major cause, which has a strong impact on the electrochemical performance of hard carbon. For example, Cao et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> showed that reducing the pyrolysis rate of hard carbon can improve the ICE due to the lower density of defects. Theoretical calculations also indicated that the vacancy defects in the carbon layer can capture Na<sup>+</sup> ions and hinder their diffusion and shuttling during the charge and discharge processes, causing irreversible capacity. Thus, many scientists have been working on developing low-defect carbon-based materials. A high carbonization temperature can increase the degree of graphitization, which is an effective way to reduce defects. Nevertheless, previous results show that continuously increasing the temperature will not always increase the ICE. For example, even though Xu et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> and Tarascon et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> raised the heat-treatment temperature of ginkgo leaves and carbon nanofibers (CNF) up to more than 2000 °C, the synthesized hard carbon demonstrated an irreversible capacity of 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Recently, Shen et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> showed that a sandwich structure composed of sucrose and graphite nanopowder could achieve an ICE of 91% owing to the high-level ordering of the structure within pseudographitic domains and decreasing the defects. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Titiric et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> also increased the ICE from 37% for pristine hard carbon to 80% through the addition of soft carbon from mesophase pitch (MP) to hard carbon from filter paper (FP) as precursors. It is believed that the soft carbon can block the open pores of hard carbon, thereby inhibiting the formation of SEI and the associated sodium-ion depletion. The current methodologies, however, are unable to fully avoid the growth of defects, and the ICE is still relatively low. Thus, a high graphite carbon structure that is highly mixed and coated on hard carbon would be perfect to decrease the defects, both on the surface and in the body of the hard carbon. Also, a clearer mechanism and an in-depth understanding of how the defects affect the irreversible consumption of sodium ions are of high significance to further improve the performance of hard carbon in SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we reported a scalable method to prepare a free-standing, low-defect hard carbon with a soft-carbon coating. This specific structure was achieved by designing a preimmersion strategy to impregnate the pitch into both the surface and the internal pores of commercial paper towels followed by an annealing process at 1200 and 1400 °C, respectively. Impressively, hard carbon impregnated by annealing at 1200 °C achieved an ultrahigh ICE of 94.1% and ultralong cycling performance (99% capacity after 100 cycles at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). For a better understanding, we also conducted a comprehensive investigation into the electrochemical mechanism of the as-prepared hard-carbon samples to unveil the mechanism for the Na<sup>+</sup>-ion storage. The results indicated that high hydrogen-containing molecules in the soft-carbon precursor can reduce the oxygen-containing groups in the hard-carbon precursor, which will decrease the probability of irreversibly capturing Na<sup>+</sup> ions by the oxygen-containing defects and additional surface terminal groups. On the other hand, the long-range-ordered soft carbon terminates the graphene edges of hard carbon, which reduces the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer formation and the subsequent consumption of sodium-related active substances during cycling. In addition, the as-prepared free-standing hard-carbon electrode could be used as a perfect model for operando Raman spectroscopy to study the sodium storage mechanism. Our results also suggested an “adsorption–intercalation–pore filling–deposition” mechanism. Sodium forms a SEI film in the first part of the slope region, which is then embedded in the turbulent layer. The plateau area mainly goes through the following two paths: (1) sodium fills in the pores near the inner wall of the carbon layer; (2) when the sodium in the inner wall pores approaches saturation, the sodium will be further deposited onto the existing sodium.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Structure and Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">In this study, we used the liquid-phase impregnation method to establish a coating of interconnected isomers between the hard carbon and the soft carbon. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, a free-standing commercial paper towel was impregnated with coal–tar pitch for 30 min and then sandwiched between two graphite layers. Then, the samples were calcined in an argon atmosphere at 1200 and 1400 °C for 3 h with the products denoted as HC-P-1200 and HC-P-1400, respectively. In order to make a better comparison, the coal–tar pitch was replaced with water as the blank control group while the other conditions remained unchanged with the products denoted as HC-W-1200 and HC-W-1400, respectively.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic diagram of the synthesis of hard carbon obtained by coal–tar–pitch impregnation/no impregnation and subsequent heat treatment at two temperatures (1200 and 1400 °C).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The structure of the above hard-carbon papers was further characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman spectroscopy. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, a characteristic broad peak appeared at 23–26°, corresponding to the (002) diffraction of graphite. The peak at 43° corresponds to the (100)/(101) peak of graphite. The (002) peak of HC-W-1400 (HC-P-1400) is shifted toward a higher angle than that of HC-W-1200 (HC-P-1200), indicating that the average intergraphene layer spacing decreases as the temperature increases. The intergraphene layer spacing can also be calculated by the Scherrer formula from the interplanar distance (<i>d</i>) value of the (002) planes (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>). The <i>d</i> values of the (002) planes for HC-W-1200 and HC-P-1200 are 0.373 and 0.369 nm, respectively. The average interlayer spacing of hard-carbon paper impregnated with pitch decreased slightly, which indicated that there was a small amount of soft carbon in HC-P-1200. The intergraphene layer spacing of the soft carbon P-1200 directly obtained from 1200 °C carbonized pitch is only 0.347 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Physical Parameters of the HC Samples from XRD<a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1-fn1" aria-label="a"><sup>a</sup></a></div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">&nbsp;</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">2θ (deg)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>d</i><sub>002</sub> (nm)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>L</i><sub>c</sub> (nm)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">fwhm (deg)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-W-1200</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">23.85</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.373</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.650</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">12.35</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-W-1400</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">25.24</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.353</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.644</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">12.5</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-P-1200</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">24.09</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" align="char" char=".">0.707</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">11.37</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-tbl1-fn1"><div class="footnote" id="tbl1-fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">fwhm: full width at half maximum.</p></div></div></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Characterization of carbon paper samples: (a) XRD patterns, (b) Raman spectra, and (c) schematic diagram of the relationship between the intensity ratios corresponding to the D band and the G band.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b shows the Raman spectrum of the hard-carbon paper described above. Compared to HC-W-1400, HC-P-1400 has a narrower G band and a more characteristic 2D peak. In addition, the 2D peak of HC-P-1200 is also somewhat more distinctive than that of HC-W-1200, which may be attributed to the soft-carbon coating from the coal–tar pitch at high temperatures. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c shows a schematic diagram of the structure corresponding to the proportions of the D1, D2, D3, D4, D5, and G bands. The Raman spectra of most of the hard carbon show common modes in the first-order region (1000–1800 cm<sup>–1</sup>), including the so-called G band around 1600 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the D band around 1350 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The G band is due to the stretching vibration of pairs of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms (E<sub>2g</sub> symmetry) and does not require the presence of aromatic rings. In fact, the position of the G band is at a wavelength of 1580 cm<sup>–1</sup> in the Raman spectrum of perfect graphite. The D band is a “disorder-induced band” arising from the presence of defects. In aromatic ring systems, both point defects and edge defects can initiate the breathing modes of sp<sup>2</sup> atoms. Thus, the D mode is forbidden in perfect graphite and becomes active when the long-range in-plane symmetry is lost. Most of the literature simply uses the intensity ratio of the D to the G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) to estimate the structural defects of the material and to calculate the length of graphite planar microcrystals (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>). Several papers have mentioned that the first-order spectrum can be further subdivided into five bands, including the already mentioned D (D1) and G bands, as well as the D2, D3, and D4 bands. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e presents the intensity ratio of the four D bands to the G band in relation to the carbon layer. The D2 band at 1620 cm<sup>–1</sup> appears on the G band, which represents a lattice vibration mode like that of the G band and generally represents the graphene layer on the surface of the graphite crystal, i.e., a graphene layer that is not directly sandwiched between another two graphene layers. In-situ Raman spectra of the Li<sup>+</sup> embedded in graphite also show a rightward shift of the G band at 1580 cm<sup>–1</sup>. This suggests that the appearance of the D2 band in hard carbon may still be owing to the interaction between the surface intersections of the two turbulent graphite layers in the stack. The D4 band at 1200 cm<sup>–1</sup> reflects the sp<sup>2</sup>–sp<sup>3</sup> hybrid structure or C–C/C═C stretching vibrations at the graphene-terminated edge. The D3 band at 1500 cm<sup>–1</sup> is the vibrational response of the short-range sp<sup>3</sup> carbon of amorphous carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> For example, some adsorbed molecules, molecular fragments, and oxygen-containing functional groups lead to the appearance of the D3 band, which is often encountered in low-temperature pyrolytic carbons (less than 1000 °C). Some reports in the literature suggest that the ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D3</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is linearly correlated with the content of oxygen-containing functional groups. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> shows the characterization of the deconvoluted Raman spectra of pitch-impregnated and unimpregnated hard-carbon paper after heat treatment at 1200 °C. The intensity of the D4 and D3 peaks of HC-P-1200 is significantly lower than that of HC-W-1200, indicating that the defects and oxygen-containing groups growing along the carbon edge or carbon layer will be reduced after the soft carbon is coated onto the hard carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a and  <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) shows that commercial paper towels with cellulose as the main component show an abundance of oxygen-containing functional groups, while coal–tar pitch has almost no such oxygen-containing bonds (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). The infrared spectrum of soft carbon obtained by direct heat treatment at the same temperature has fewer oxygen-containing functional groups than cellulose-based hard carbon (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). Therefore, the reduction of defects may be due to the good coating of coal–tar pitch on the paper, which can inhibit the formation of oxygen-containing functional groups at the edges and interlayers of hard carbon during carbonization (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). In addition, the carbon paper coated with pitch (HC-P-1200 and HC-P-1400) has a higher G peak (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c and  <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> d), indicating that the carbon paper impregnated with coal–tar pitch contains more long-range graphite domains with smaller intergraphene spacing. According to Eckmann et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> the area ratio (<i>A</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub>) of these peaks can be considered to replace the intensity ratio to calculate <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> due to the change in the peak width. In addition, according to the value of <i>A</i><sub>D1</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub>, the in-plane grain size <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> can be calculated by the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>, as reported based on the Tuinstra–Koenig relationship.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Deconvoluted Raman spectra for (a) HC-W-1200, (b) HC-P-1200, (c) HC-W-1400, and (d) HC-P-1400. (e) FTIR spectra of commercial paper towels and coal pitch.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a> lists the values of <i>A</i><sub>D1</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub>, <i>A</i><sub>D2</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub>, <i>A</i><sub>D3</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub>, <i>A</i><sub>D4</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub>, and <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> after deconvolution of the Raman spectra for the four carbon paper materials listed above. We also present these values on a line graph (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c) and correlate these ratios to the electrochemical properties of the hard carbon.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. Physical Parameters of the HC Samples from the Raman Spectra</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-W-1200</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.262</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char"
