rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.234</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">15.88</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,  <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b,  <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, and  <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d shows scanning electron microscopy images (SEM) of HC-W-1200, HC-P-1200, HC-W-1400, and HC-P-1400, respectively. The structure of the hard-carbon paper is composed of mutually entangled fibers with a length of approximately 50 μm. These fibers clearly inherit the morphological characteristics of the raw materials. The surface of the pitch-treated carbon HC-P-1200 is relatively rough with some particles loaded on the fiber strips. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1c–f</a> in the Supporting Information shows a higher magnification of the SEM information. This may be due to the supersaturation of the pitch, which causes the soft-carbon particles to form on the residual carbon paper at high temperatures. Among them, the fiber surfaces of HC-W-1200 and HC-W-1400 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1c and S1e</a>) are relatively smooth. Many natural grooves and grooves on the surfaces of carbon paper single fibers may more easily come into contact with the electrolyte and Na<sup>+</sup> ions, thus promoting the transport and diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f comparesthe high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images of two kinds of carbon paper, HC-W-1200 and HC-P-1200. HC-W-1200 exhibits a highly disordered structure with a shorter carbon layer. HC-P-1200 has clearer graphene lattice stripes, and a longer carbon layer is distributed around the short-range-disordered carbon layer. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>i–l shows schematic diagrams of the structures of these four kinds of carbon. HC-W-1200, which is not impregnated by pitch, shows more defects (represented by red dots). Compared with HC-W-1200, the oxygen-containing defects of HC-P-1200 will be reduced (with the red spots becoming gray), and the long-range-ordered soft carbon after carbonization insulates the contact between the terminal edge of graphene and the electrolyte. HC-W-1400 and HC-P-1400 show similar behavior, but their graphitization degree is increased by heat treatment at 1400 °C. The HRTEM images of HC-W-1400 and HC-P-1400 show clearer long-range graphite domains and smaller intergraphene spacing (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>g and  <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>h). HC-P-1400, however, failed to form a good soft-carbon-coated hard-carbon isomerization mesophase, which may be due to the higher temperature, leading to the formation of longer graphite domains of hard carbon, which are more difficult to coat with soft carbon.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Characterization of carbon paper samples. SEM of images of (a) HC-W-1200, (b) HC-P-1200, (c) HC-W-1400, and (d) HC-P-1400. HRTEM of images of (e) HC-W-1200, (f) HC-P-1200, (g) HC-W-1400, and (h) HC-P-1400. Structural diagrams of (i) HC-1200, (j) HC-P-1200, (k) HC-W-1400, and (l) HC-P-1400 (red dot represents the defect, and yellow line represents the carbonized product of coal tar pitch, which will be arranged between the adjacent the short-range carbon layers).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.2.  Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance relevant to sodium storage in the free-standing hard-carbon electrodes is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, which shows the galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the four electrodes at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The reversible capacity of the HC-P-1200, HC-W-1200, HC-P-1400, and HC-W-1400 electrodes is 293.3, 297.4, 253.1, and 247.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The HC-P-1200 electrode has the highest ICE at 94.4%, which is greater than the 87.2% of the HC-W-1200 electrode. All of the curves show a sloping region for the high potential range (2.0–0.1 V) and a plateau region for the low potential range (0.1–0.001 V). Specifically, the carbon papers heat treated at 1400 °C (HC-W-1400 and HC-P-1400) have a steeper slope than those heat treated at 1200 °C (HC-W-1200 and HC-P-1200). The percentage of slope capacity for HC-P-1200 is 35.6%, while that for HC-W-1400 drops to 21.2% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). Some reports in the literature suggest that the slope is due to adsorption and that more defects on the surface are responsible for the adsorption capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> It can be observed, however, that the oxygen-containing defects (corresponding to <i>A</i><sub>D3</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f) and graphene terminal edge defects (corresponding to <i>A</i><sub>D3</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f) are smaller for HC-P-1200 compared to HC-W-1400. This suggests that the slope is not only due to adsorption, since if the slope capacity were simply adsorption the slope capacity of HC-P-1200 would be smaller than that of HC-W-1400. In addition, the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> values of HC-W-1400 and HC-P-1400 are smaller than that for HC-W-1200, as described above. Therefore, the carbon treated at 1400 °C shows less slope capacity, which may be mainly due to its smaller <i>d</i> spacing. In addition, for many large layer spacings of expanded graphite, only a slope capacity can be observed. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> This also indirectly suggests that embedding of Na<sup>+</sup> into the larger graphite interlayers is also occurring in the slope region. This simple comparison also supports the “three-stage” mechanism (adsorption–embedding–pore filling) proposed by other reports in the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 23 to 27">(23−27)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the 1st, 2nd, 10th, and 100th cycles of the various carbon papers: (a) HC-W-1200, HC-W-1400, HC-P-1200, and HC-P-1400. (b) Contribution ratios of the slope capacity and plateau capacity in the second cycle discharge curves. (c) Area ratios of the four D bands to the G band of the integrated Raman peaks of carbon paper in the different samples. (d) Charge and discharge curves of HC-P-1200 under different current densities. (e) Comparison of the cycle performance of various carbon papers.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d shows the rate performance and retention rates of various materials. HC-P-1200 has the best rate performance and rate recovery performance. It can still maintain a reversible specific capacity of 122.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>). When the current density returns to 20 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity of HC-P-1200 recovers to 100% of the initial reversible capacity. The recovery rate of the electrode (HC-P-1400) carbonized at 1400 °C after pitch treatment also exceeds 100%. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e shows the capacity retention of the HC-P-1200, HC-W-1200, HC-P-1400, and HC-W-1400 electrodes at 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 100 cycles. The HC-P-1200 electrode delivered a high reversible capacity of 293.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the second cycle, and the capacity retention was 99% after 100 cycles, which indicates an ultrastable cycling performance. The HC-W-1200 electrode, however, only delivered a capacity of 266.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles. The <i>A</i><sub>D3</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub> and <i>A</i><sub>D4</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub> ratios of HC-W-1200 are significantly higher than those of HC-P-1200 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), indicating that HC-1200 without the pitch coating has higher oxygen defects and graphene edge defects. During cycling, these defects may repeatedly form and consume the SEI, causing a loss of active materials and thus reducing the cycling performance. In contrast, the pitch-pretreated HC-P-1200 with a reduced composition of oxygen-containing defects and graphene edge defects maintained good cycling performance despite its lower initial reversible capacity. In contrast, after carbon heat treatment at 1400 °C, the specific discharge capacities of HC-W-1400 and HC-P-1400 after 100 cycles were 204.2 and 236.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with capacity retention rates of 81.6% and 91.6%, respectively. The cycling performance of free-standing hard-carbon paper heat treated at 1400 °C is worse than that of hard-carbon paper heat treated at 1200 °C, which may be due to the worse kinetics of sodium-ion transfer in the plateau region, resulting in greater polarization after long-term cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of sodium insertion and sodium removal for the various carbon papers. HC-P-1200 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a) and HC-W-1200 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b) have similar CV curves, and their first scan curves show sharp reduction peaks near 0.9 and 0.38 V, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The reduction peak near 0.2–0.4 V results from formation of the SEI film. The CV curves of HC-W-1400 and HC-P-1400 are very different from those of the carbon treated at 1200 °C because they have only one reduction peak in the 0.4–0.2 V range, and the reduction peak in the high potential range disappears. The reason may be that the higher treatment temperature causes oxygen defects and reduction of the specific surface area. The irreversible reduction peak near 0.3 V is caused by the decomposition of the electrolyte’s solvent molecules ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) into films at the edges of the graphene. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8e and S8f</a> shows the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis of different potentials during the first cycle discharge of HC-P-1200 and HC-W-1200, respectively. HC-P-1200 has a lower SEI layer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) than HC-W-1200 in the process of discharge to 0.6, 0.4, and 0.1 V, which indicates that the SEI film formed by HC-P-1200 is thinner.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. CV curves of (a) HC-P-1200, (b) HC-W-1200, (c) HC-P-1400, and (d) HC-W-1400 in Na cells with enlargements in the insets. Electrolyte: 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC:DMC (1:1 wt %). Scan rate: 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Potential range: 0–2.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The 2032-type full cells were assembled with hard-carbon paper HC-P-1200 electrode as the anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as the cathode. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a, the average voltage of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub> (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//HC-P-1200 full battery was 3.3 V, and its cumulative discharge capacity was 275.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (based on the mass load of the HC-P-1200 anode). The capacity retention rate of the full battery was 73.12% after 50 cycles at a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. To demonstrate the large-scale preparation of carbon paper, we also prepared a 20 cm × 40 cm carbon paper using a large vacuum box furnace (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a and S2b</a>). The prepared carbon paper had good flexibility because it did not break, even if it was bent to 180° (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>). The preparation strategy for hard-carbon paper with comprehensive electrochemical properties can offer great inspiration to the commercialization of hard carbon. In addition, the self-supporting hard-carbon paper that we synthesized does not have toxic inactive substances and eliminates the cost of the current collector, while the size of the self-supporting hard-carbon paper is adjustable, so that it can be directly produced for the laminated assembly of pouch batteries, which will greatly increase the volumetric energy density of the system. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b, the full battery has a good cycle performance with a retention rate of 74% and a cycle of 60 cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) First and second cycles of the full cell based on Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//HC-P-1200. (b) Cycling performance of the full cell.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">2.3.  Elucidating the Sodium Storage Mechanism by In-Situ/Operando Raman Spectroscopy</h3><div class="NLM_p">The use of free-standing hard-carbon paper to study sodium storage mechanisms has the great advantage of not requiring the interference of conductive agents, current collectors, and binders. Therefore, the free-standing hard-carbon paper can be installed directly in the in-situ cell for testing. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a shows the operando Raman spectroscopy cell configuration provided by Beijing SciStar Technology Co. Ltd.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) In-situ/Operando Raman spectroscopy cell configuration used in this study. (b) In-situ Raman spectroscopy of HC-P-1200</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b shows the in-situ Raman curve of HC-P-1200. The test method is to manually test the Raman information at a fixed potential. In order to better compare the peak shape and peak position, we performed a unified normalization process. It can be seen that for the initial potential, that is, before any sodium is inserted, the typical spectrum of hard carbon is observed. The insertion of sodium obviously leads to a gradual change in the Raman spectrum. The main feature of the spectrum at low sodium content is the peaks near 1600 and 1350 cm<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to the G- and D-band modes of the graphite mentioned above. As the discharge progresses, the positions of the G peak and the D peak slowly move to a low wavenumber and the peak shape becomes very wide, and the G peak is extremely inconspicuous after the 0.1 V plateau region. The decrease in the position of the gradual (reversible) G peak can be understood as the electron doping caused by the gradual addition and uniform diffusion of sodium in each layer of sodium. In order to test the operando changes of hard-carbon storage more carefully, we adopt automatic collection. See the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a> for specific steps.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a</a> shows the actual test curves of operando Raman spectroscopy. During discharge from 1.5 to 0.1 V, the position of the G peak will slowly move to lower wavenumber, and the peak and its width will gradually change until the plateau area after 0.1 V where they almost disappear. In addition, the intensity and background of the Raman curve continuously increased by 0.25–0.031 V during the discharge, but the total intensity of the Raman curve suddenly dropped from 0.031 to −0.001 V. During charging, the peak intensity from 0.021 to 0.063 V is low, but it increases sharply after 0.075 V and then decreases continuously after from 0.11 to 1.0 V during charging.</div><div class="NLM_p">This change does not seem to be a random accident. We tried to match the intensity and background slope of the D/G peaks of the Raman spectrum with the potential change during charge and discharge (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a). The background slope and the intensity of the D/G peak data points showed periodic and reversible changes during the first and second cycles of charge and discharge. The D/G peak intensity and background slope did not change much in the slope area, in which there was a slight increase between 1.5 and 0.8 V and no change between 0.8 and 0.5 V. The latter range corresponds to the SEI film formation process. Between 0.5 and 0.1 V, the intensity and background slope clearly increase, which corresponds to the process of sodium-ion intercalation. The hard carbon (HC-P-1200) designed according to this paper has very low defects and a high ICE. The defects of HC-P-1200 mentioned above are fewer than those of HC-W-1400, but this electrode shows a higher slope capacity. Therefore, the capacity contribution of the slope between 0.5 and 0.1 V cannot be only attributed to adsorption. The sodium adsorbed on the tube surface can also cause the G peak to shift to a lower wavenumber. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> If adsorption were the only mechanism, the G peak would split into two peaks because of the adsorbed part and the unabsorbed graphene layer, but this phenomenon has not been found in this work. In addition, because the number of hard-carbon turbulent layers in this paper is about three, it will not form a multiorder graphite intercalation compound (GIC) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 30 to 33">(30−33)</a> as lithium intercalates into the graphite. Therefore, the Raman spectrum does not show splitting into two peaks due to adjacent intercalated and nonintercalated graphene layers. The shape and position of the G peak in the plateau area are almost unchanged, indicating that there is no further insertion. This may be related to the gradual increase in the metallic properties of sodium substances observed by <sup>23</sup>Na nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The sharp increase of the background slope and D/G peak intensity at this stage may also be related to sodium filling. In addition, the disappearance of the G peak was also observed in the last stage of Li intercalation into graphite, which is due to the high electrical conductivity of the GIC, which leads to a decrease in the thickness of the optical skin and low intensity of Raman scattering in this stage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/medium/am1c12171_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. (a) GITT test of the first charge and discharge and the discharge in the second ring slope region. (b) Charge and discharge of the first cycle and the D/G peak intensity of the slope section of the second cycle, which change periodically with the background slope (change of the background slope and G peak intensity of the plateau area during charge–discharge). (c) Schematic diagram of the sodium storage stages of hard carbon.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/asset/images/large/am1c12171_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12171&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition, we also found that after the first discharge to 0.03 V, the background slope and the intensity of the D/G peaks decreased sharply until the cell was charged to 0.004 V. After that, during the charging process from 0.04 to 0.12 V, the background slope and the intensity of the D/G peaks increased sharply. After charging from 0.12 V, this number dropped sharply. The dramatic changes in the Raman intensity and background slope in the plateau area seem to be interesting. We also conducted galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) testing during the charge–discharge process and found that the change in the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> in the plateau region was completely opposite to the change in the intensity of the D/G peaks and the background slope measured by Raman spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>b). At present, the explanation for the sudden increase of the diffusion coefficient in this section is also controversial. For example, Ji et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> thought that this was due to the adsorption of Na atoms on the pore surface. Cao et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> believes, however, that this change should be attributed to the highly attractive interaction between the Na ions inserted in the single-phase NaC<sub><i>x</i></sub> compounds. Huang et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> conducted GITT testing of a series of phenolic resin-based hard-carbon samples and concluded that the sudden increase in the diffusion coefficient is due to sodium filling in the closed pores. Here, we attempt to propose a new sodium storage mechanism, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>c. First, the slope region corresponds to part of the irreversible adsorption and the film formation process of the SEI, the 0.6–0.1 V region corresponds to the intercalation of sodium ions, and the region below 0.1 V corresponds to the filling of the pores with Na<sup>+</sup> ions.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The sodium storage mechanism in the plateau area is still controversial, particularly because the GITT observations showed that the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> increased in the last part of the plateau area, which could not be explained. The relationship between the changes in the in-situ Raman spectra observed in this paper and the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient measured by GITT seems to explain this problem, however. We propose that the pore filling in the plateau area will be divided into two parts: (1) sodium first fills the pores near the inner wall of the carbon layer; (2) when the inner wall pores are nearly saturated with sodium, the existing sodium will further adsorb the filled sodium, which is similar to the normal deposition process for sodium. This seems to be a good explanation for the increase in the diffusion coefficient due to the easy adsorption between sodium and sodium. On the contrary, the extremely difficult process of desorption of sodium from sodium leads to a sharp decrease in the diffusion coefficient. Since it is easier for sodium to desorb from the carbon surface of the inner walls of the pores, the diffusion coefficient begins to increase again. In the slope region, the separation of sodium from the graphene interlayers is relatively easy, which causes the diffusion coefficient to decrease relatively gently. The exact demonstration of the mechanism will be explained in a subsequent paper on the design of a high-capacity and full-plateau hard carbon.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">3.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this paper, a paper towel impregnated with coal–tar pitch was used as the precursor to prepare self-standing hard-carbon paper electrodes for sodium-ion batteries. The prepared HC-P-1200 formed a uniform hard-carbon@soft-carbon heterogeneous mesophase structure. The HC-P-1200 electrode showed a high ICE of 94.1% and ultralong cycling stability (99% retention after 100 cycles with current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). The reason for the increase in the ICE of hard carbon impregnated with pitch is that in the process of carbonization, molecules with a high H/C ratio can reduce oxygen defects in hard carbon with a high O/C ratio and form long-range-ordered soft-carbon components to isolate the contact between the terminal edge of graphene and the electrolyte. The reduction of oxygen-containing defects inhibits the irreversible capture of sodium ions by defects. Isolation of the graphene terminal edge from the electrolyte inhibits the consumption and dissolution of the SEI in the initial and subsequent cycles. In addition, the self-supporting hard-carbon paper was also used to study the mechanism of sodium storage by operando Raman spectroscopy. Our sodium storage mechanism extends the “adsorption–intercalation–pore filling–deposition” model. We focus on the reasons for the increase of the sodium-ion diffusion coefficient in the latter part of the plateau region and propose for the first time that the pore filling in the plateau area will be divided into two parts: (1) sodium will be filled into the pores near the inner wall of the carbon layer; (2) when the sodium in the inner wall pores is close to saturation, the sodium will be further deposited onto the existing sodium.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">4.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">4.1.  Materials Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Commercial paper towels were obtained from Dongguan Guixiang Paper Products Co., Ltd. The medium-temperature coal pitch powder was dissolved in dimethylformamide (DMF) to prepare a 0.02 g L<sup>–1</sup> solution. Then, the paper towels were cut into 1.5 cm × 6 cm rectangles. For comparison, the cut paper pieces were immersed in deionized water and a medium-temperature coal pitch solution and then sonicated for 0.5 h. The cut paper was sandwiched between two graphite plates and placed in a tube furnace. Under an argon atmosphere, the paper was raised to the target temperature (1200 and 1400 °C) at 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> and then kept at 1200 °C for 2 h. Finally, the carbon paper between the graphite plates was taken out, and then discs with a radius of 0.5 cm were cut out with a punch. The synthesized carbon paper is denoted by the annealing temperature in the last 4 digits, HC-P-1200, HC-P-1400, and HC-W-1200, and HC-W-1400. P is to designate the samples using the coal–tar pitch coating.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">4.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The synthesized material was characterized by X-ray diffraction (18 kW D/MAX2500 V Rigaku Co., Japan). The morphology was observed with a scanning electron microscope (HITACHI SU-1500). In order to analyze the detailed crystal structure of the material, a high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM; FEI Tecnai F20) was used. FTIR spectra were recorded on an FTIR spectrometer (Thermo Scientific Nicolet iS5). Raman spectra were recorded with a Renishaw inVia instrument equipped with a 532 nm wavelength laser. While conducting the operando Raman spectroscopy, the charge and discharge curves versus time of the in-situ cell were collected by the chronopotentiometry facility on a CHI660E electrochemical workstation. Operando Raman spectroscopy equipment was provided by Beijing SciStar Technology Co. Ltd. The mass of the active material was 0.753 mg, and the current was set to 0.0452 mA, which was equivalent to a current density of 60 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The potential range was from 2.0 to −0.003 V. Scan interval: 434 s. Total test: 80 points. Exposure time: 10 s. Laser power: 50%. Accumulation: 20 times. In-Situ Raman test method is to pause at each potential point and perform manual test to collect data. In order to better compare the peak shape and peak position, we have carried out a unified normalization process.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">4.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Electrochemical performance testing was carried out on button battery cells (CR2032). The sodium block was rolled into a film with a thickness of 0.1 mm through a paster machine and then cut into discs with a diameter of 1.2 cm as the counter electrodes. Glass fiber (GF/A, Whatman, UK) was used for the separators, and the electrolyte was 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in 1:1 EC/DMC (where EC is ethylene carbonate and DMC is dimethyl carbonate). According to the normal button assembly sequence, the cut carbon paper (loading amount is 1–1.2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>) and the electrolyte, diaphragm, and sodium sheet mentioned above were assembled in a glovebox filled with argon 17/5000 (H<sub>2</sub>O, O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.01 ppm). The galvanostatic charge and discharge testing was carried out on a LAND CT2001 battery test system at room temperature in the potential range of 0–2.0 V (relative to Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was conducted using a CHI660E electrochemical workstation with a sweep rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and a voltage range from 0 to 2.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i15">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c12171?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c12171</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Digital picture of the material sandwiched between graphite boards, and SEM image of the carbon paper; digital photograph of large size carbon paper; comparison of XRD, Raman, and infrared spectra of soft carbon; rate performance of HC-P-1200; Nyquist plots of HC-P-1200 and HC-W-1200; full information on operando Raman spectra of the charge–discharge process; periodic variation of the intensity of the charge–discharge for the first cycle, and slope of the second cycle at 1000 and 2000 cm<sup>–1</sup> Raman shift (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf">am1c12171_si_001.pdf (1.18 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c12171" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Conversion reactions for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Klein, Franziska; Jache, Birte; Bhide, Amrtha; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">38</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15876-15887</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Research on Na-ion batteries is focused on finding suitable electrode materials that enable cell reactions of high energy densities combined with low cost.  Naturally, an assessment of potential electrode materials requires a rational comparison with the analog reaction in Li-ion batteries.  The authors discuss the broad range of different conversion reactions for Na-ion batteries based on their basic thermodn. properties and compare them with their Li analogs.  Capacities, voltages, energy densities and vol. expansions are summarized to sketch out the scope for future studies in this research field.  For a given conversion electrode material, replacing Li by Na leads to a const. shift in cell potential ΔEo(Li-Na) depending on the material class.  For chlorides ΔEo(Li-Na) is nearly zero.  The theor. energy densities of conversion reactions of Na with fluorides or chlorides as pos. electrode materials typically reach values between 700 W h kg-1 and 1000 W h kg-1.  Next to the thermodn. assessment, results on several conversion reactions between Cu compds. (CuS, CuO, CuCl, CuCl2) and Na are being discussed.  Reactions with CuS and CuO were chosen because these compds. are frequently studied for conversion reactions with Li.  Chlorides are interesting because of ΔEo(Li-Na) ≈ 0 V As a result of chloride soly. in the electrolyte, the conversion process proceeds at defined potentials under rather small kinetic limitations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvFdD49Ctj47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liy6jv-otj2Vg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsVWrtbrE&amp;md5=7135136abf30b3a83827d65de85c8f45</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201804458"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">An, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanostructured Conversion-Type Negative Electrode Materials for Low-Cost and High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical performance of porous carbon/tin composite anodes for sodium-ion and lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Yunhua; Zhu, Yujie; Liu, Yihang; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">128-133</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.  Understanding of the reaction mechanism between Sn and Na ions will provide insight towards exploring and designing new alloy-based anode materials for Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqL8pwZXJa53LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg8HVkDi6gxQg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsFKhtr8%253D&amp;md5=7fba4efb710816f1ca877f0494169a46</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201808745"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, H.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent progress of emerging cathode materials for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Jun; Li, Xiao; Tang, Kaikai; Wang, Dandan; Long, Mengqi; Gao, Hong; Chen, Weihua; Liu, Chuntai; Liu, Hao; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Chemistry Frontiers</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3735-3764</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MCFAC5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2052-1537</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Recently, due to the excessive consumption of fossil energy and the intermittent nature of clean energy resources, electricity storage has attracted great attention from both academia and industry.  Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) with high energy d. have been widely used for portable energy storage systems.  However, limited lithium resources cannot meet the demands for large-scale applications, esp. the grid-scale energy storage.  Owing to the abundant natural resources of sodium and the low price, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have become potential substitutes for LIBs.  Cathode materials are key components that det. the overall performance and entire cost of SIBs.  However, the unstable structure and limited electrochem. performance make them difficult to meet the requirement of practical applications.  This review summarizes the recent development of the cathode materials used for SIBs, and the modified strategies applied to improve their electrochem. performance.  Finally, crit. challenges and perspectives to promote the development of SIBs are also presented.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp8ZUfRDzVosbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg8HVkDi6gxQg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXmvVymtrw%253D&amp;md5=6a905d575e4f69f242f907539d23eada</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201400794"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sn4+xP3 amorphous Sn-P composites as anodes for sodium-ion batteries with low cost, high capacity, long Life, and superior rate capability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Weijie; Chou, Shu-Lei; Wang, Jia-Zhao; Kim, Jung Ho; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">24</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4037-4042</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn4+xP3@(Sn-P) composite was prepd. in large quantities by direct low-speed ball milling of the P and Snpowders and studied its electrochem. performance as anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  The results demonstrated that the Sn4+xP3·(Sn-P) electrode using CMC binder delivered an initial discharge capacity of 1030 mA h g-1 and a reversible charge capacity of 892 mA h g-1, at the c.d. of 100 mA g-1.  The addn. of 5% FEC to the electrolyte can improve the cycling stability of the Sn4+xP3 electrode exhibited superior high rate capability, with a stable capacity of 165 mA h g-1 at the 10 C rate (5000 mA g-1).  All the resultes indicate that Sn4+xP3·(Sn-P) would be a promising anode material candidate for sodium-ion batteries with low cost, long cycling stability, and relatively good rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqoO0b7cRbjN7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhXF44Z5HPizQ"> &gt;&gt;                 10.1002/aenm.202002704</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D11%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D2002704%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DL.-F.%2BZhao%26author%3DZ.%2BHu%26author%3DW.-H.%2BLai%26author%3DY.%2BTao%26author%3DJ.%2BPeng%26author%3DZ.-C.%2BMiao%26author%3DY.-X.%2BWang%26author%3DS.-L.%2BChou%26author%3DH.-K.%2BLiu%26author%3DS.-X.%2BDou%26title%3DHard%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%253A%2BFundamental%2BUnderstanding%2Band%2BCommercial%2BPerspectives%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%2Bbeyond%2BLi-Ion%2Band%2BK-Ion%2BCounterparts%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.202002704&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c12171&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.202002704&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard Carbon Anodes: Fundamental Understanding and Commercial Perspectives for Na-Ion Batteries beyond Li-Ion and K-Ion Counterparts</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Ling-Fei; Hu, Zhe; Lai, Wei-Hong; Tao, Ying; Peng, Jian; Miao, Zong-Cheng; Wang, Yun-Xiao; Chou, Shu-Lei; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2002704</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Hard carbon (HC) is recognized as a promising anode material with outstanding electrochem. performance for alkali metal-ion batteries including lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), as well as their analogs sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs).  Herein, a comprehensive review of the recent research is presented to interpret the challenges and opportunities for the applications of HC anodes.  The ion storage mechanisms, materials design, and electrolyte optimizations for alkali metal-ion batteries are illustrated in-depth.  HC is particularly promising as an anode material for SIBs.  The solid-electrolyte interphase, initial Coulombic efficiency, safety concerns, and all-climate performances, which are vital for practical applications, are comprehensively discussed.  Furthermore, com. prototypes of SIBs based on HC anodes are extensively elaborated.  The remaining challenges and research perspectives are provided, aiming to shed light on future research and early commercialization of HC-based SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrgUes-Iv_xOrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhXF44Z5HPizQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXisVagtLrM&amp;md5=dc06d1bd2c8724df623e45590cac38e2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703238"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L. Spectral analysis and structural information</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sadezky, A.; Muckenhuber, H.; Grothe, H.; Niessner, R.; Poeschl, U.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2005</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">43</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1731-1742</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Exptl. conditions and math. fitting procedures for the collection and anal. of Raman spectra of soot and related carbonaceous materials were investigated and optimized with a Raman microscope system operated at 3 different laser excitation wavelengths (514, 633, and 780 nm).  Several band combinations for spectral anal. were tested, and a combination of 4 Lorentzian-shaped bands (G, D1, D2, D4) at about 1580, 1350, 1620, and 1200 cm-1, resp., with a Gaussian-shaped band (D3) at ∼1500 cm-1 was best suited for the 1st-order spectra.  The 2nd-order spectra were best fitted with Lorentzian-shaped bands at about 2450, 2700, 2900, and 3100 cm-1.  Spectral parameters (band positions, full widths at half max., and intensity ratios) are reported for several types of industrial C black (Degussa Printex, Cabot Monarch), diesel soot (particulate matter from modern heavy duty vehicle and passenger car engine exhaust, NIST SRM1650), spark-discharge soot (Palas GfG100), and graphite.  Several parameters, in particular the width of the D1 band at ∼1350 cm-1, provide structural information and allow to discriminate the sample materials, but the characterization and distinction of different types of soot is limited by the exptl. reproducibility of the spectra and the statistical uncertainties of curve fitting.  The results are discussed and compared with x-ray diffraction measurements and earlier Raman spectroscopic studies of comparable materials, where different measurement and fitting procedures was applied.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqI47SxmqUfxrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgKHL-yFKuHBg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2MXltFGrtLc%253D&amp;md5=045e6d9ee2eb3d84bd001fca25bc7f62</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.3390/c1010077"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bokobza, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bruneel, J.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Couzi, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Raman Spectra of Carbon-Based Materials (from Graphite to Carbon Black) and of Some Silicone Composites</span>. <i>C J.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comparison of Raman signatures and laser-induced incandescence with direct numerical simulation of soot growth in non-premixed ethylene/air flames</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Herdman, Jennifer D.; Connelly, Blair C.; Smooke, Mitchell D.; Long, Marshall B.; Miller, J. Houston</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">15</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5298-5311</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Predicted soot concns. from numerical simulations of N2-dild., ethylene/air flames were compared with laser-induced incandescence and Raman spectra obsd. from samples thermophoretically extd. using a rapid insertion technique.  In some flame regions, Raman spectra were obscured by intense radiation which appeared to peak in the near IR spectral region.  There was good agreement between spatial profiles of this ex-situ, laser-induced incandescence (ES-LII) and traditional in-situ, laser-induced incandescence (IS-LII).  Raman signatures were obsd. from low in the flame and extended into the upper flame regions.  Spectra consisted of overlapping bands between 1000 and 2000 cm-1, dominated by the G band near ≈1580 cm-1 and the D band in the upper 1300 cm-1 range.  Several routines were examd. to de-convolve the data, including 3- and 5-band models and a 2-band Breit-Wigner-Fano model.  Since Raman signals were obsd. at heights below those where in-situ LII was obsd., it was postulated that these signals may be attributable to smaller particles.  Results suggested the obsd. Raman signals were attributable to particles with modest (≈1 nm) crystallite sizes.  This observation is discussed in the context of current models for nascent particle formation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr8faPc4-kllbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgKHL-yFKuHBg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1aiurfM&amp;md5=2b8978c91bf31adb5c62995def6c646e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1021/nl300901a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Eckmann, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Felten, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mishchenko, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Britnell, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Krupke, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Novoselov, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Raman spectroscopy is able to probe disorder in graphene through defect-activated peaks.  It is of great interest to link these features to the nature of disorder.  Here the authors present a detailed anal. of the Raman spectra of graphene contg. different type of defects.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, Yang; He, Kai; Zhu, Yujie; Han, Fudong; Xu, Yunhua; Matsuda, Isamu; Ishii, Yoshitaka; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrFEZ8wJYk1irVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgKHL-yFKuHBg"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode: Tunable Local Structures and Sodium Storage Mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Yu; Sun, Shixiong; Ou, Mingyang; Liu, Yi; Fan, Chenyang; Sun, Xueping; Peng, Jian; Li, Yuyu; Qiu, Yuegang; Wei, Peng; Deng, Zhi; Xu, Yue; Han, Jiantao; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon (HC) is one of the most promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its suitable potential and high reversible capacity.  At the same time, the correlation between carbon local structure and sodium-ion storage behavior is not clearly understood.  In this paper, the two series of HC materials with perfect spherical morphol. and tailored microstructures were designed and successfully produced using resorcinol formaldehyde (RF) resin as precursor.  Via hydrothermal self-assembly and controlled pyrolysis, RF is a flexible precursor for high-purity carbon with a wide range of local-structure variation.  Using these processes, one series of five representative RF-based HC nanospheres with varying degrees of graphitization were obtained from an RF precursor at different carbonization temps.  The other series of HC materials with various microscopic carbon layer lengths and shapes was achieved by carbonizing five RF precursors with different crosslinking degrees at a single carbonization condition (1300 °C and 2 h).  On the basis of the microstructures, unique electrochem. characteristics, and at. pair distribution function (PDF) analyses, we proposed a new model of "three-phase" structural for HC materials and found triregion Na-ion storage behavior: chemi-/physisorption, intercalation between carbon layers, and pore-filling, derived from the HC phases, resp.  These results enable new understanding and insight into the sodium storage mechanism in HC materials and improve the potential for carbon-based SIB anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpYKW2rHattOLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhXbh1oMLR4UQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXotlOnsrY%253D&amp;md5=ee7aac51818212fc92f6fc3ba5c340d8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.8b01761"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Anji

                        cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insight into Sodium Insertion and the Storage Mechanism in Hard Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Anji Reddy, M.; Helen, M.; Gross, Axel; Fichtner, Maximilian; Euchner, Holger</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">While the technol. importance of carbon-based anodes for sodium-ion batteries is undebated, the underlying mechanism for sodium insertion and storage is still strongly disputed.  Here, a joint exptl. and theor. study is presented that allows to provide detailed insights into the process of Na insertion in nongraphitizable (hard) carbon.  For this purpose, the data from in situ Raman scattering of Na insertion in hard carbon are combined with d. functional theory-based lattice dynamics and band structure calcns. for Na insertion in graphitic model structures used for a local description of graphitic domains in hard carbon.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revealing sodium ion storage mechanism in hard carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alvin, Stevanus; Yoon, Dohyeon; Chandra, Christian; Cahyadi, Handi Setiadi; Park, Jae-Ho; Chang, Wonyoung; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Kim, Jaehoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">145</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">67-81</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although many studies have demonstrated the excellent potential of hard carbon as an anode in sodium ion batteries, the contribution of its active sites to the capacities of the sloping and plateau voltage regions is not yet clear.  Herein, systematical investigation of the relationship between the active sites and sodium ion (Na+) storage in the sloping and plateau voltage regions was presented.  In light of the physicochem. properties of the lignin-derived hard carbon (graphitization degree, interlayer spacing, micropore size distribution, and sp. surface area), the results of Na+ ion diffusivity, and the change in these properties during Na+ ion insertion/extn. (as characterized by ex situ techniques), new mechanistic insights into Na+ ion storage were proposed.  At the beginning of the sodiation process, Na+ ions were adsorbed on defect/edge sites; then partial micropore filling occurred in the sloping region above 0.1 V.  In the plateau region below 0.1 V, Na+ ions were intercalated in the graphitic layers, and further adsorption in the micropores occurred near the cutoff potential.  Furthermore, sodium clustering occurred below 0.1 V owing to the high concn. of Na+ ions in the micropores.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoGLimnY898qLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhXbh1oMLR4UQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhtVarsLs%253D&amp;md5=38b3afcef22cf445274c04bcd7f2c3fe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.0c00867"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weaving, J.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Elucidating the Sodiation Mechanism in Hard Carbon by Operando Raman Spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Weaving, Julia S.; Lim, Alvin; Millichamp, Jason; Neville, Tobias P.; Ledwoch, Daniela; Kendrick, Emma; McMillan, Paul F.; Shearing, Paul R.; Howard, Christopher A.; Brett, Dan J. L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7474-7484</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAEMCQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2574-0962</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Operando microbeam Raman spectroscopy is used to map the changes in hard carbon during sodiation and desodiation in unprecedented detail, elucidating several important and unresolved aspects of the sodiation mechanism.  On sodiation a substantial, reversible decrease in G-peak energy is obsd., which corresponds directly to the sloping part of the voltage profile and we argue can only be due to steady intercalation of sodium between the turbostratic layers of the hard carbon.  The corresponding reversibility of the D-peak energy change is consistent with intercalation rather than representing a permanent increase in disorder.  No change in energy of the graphitic phonons occurs over the low-voltage plateau, indicating that intercalation sats. before sodium clusters form in micropores in this region.  At the start of the initial sodiation there is no change in G- and D-peak energy as the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) forms.  After SEI formation, the background slope of the spectra increases irreversibly due to fluorescence.  The importance of in situ/operando expts. over ex situ studies is demonstrated; washing the samples or air exposure causes the G- and D-peaks to revert back to their original states because of SEI removal and sodium deintercalation and confirms no permanent damage to the carbon structure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoEDvtHvHdASrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhXbh1oMLR4UQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsFGks77E&amp;md5=c8fdf72638e2a4b8bfa2b6c3e6c0ce2c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1039/D0EE01363C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Au, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alptekin, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jensen, A.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A revised mechanistic model for sodium insertion in hard carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Au, Heather; Alptekin, Hande; Jensen, Anders C. S.; Olsson, Emilia; O'Keefe, Christopher A.; Smith, Thomas; Crespo-Ribadeneyra, Maria; Headen, Thomas F.; Grey, Clare P.; Cai, Qiong; Drew, Alan J.; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons have shown considerable promise as anodes for emerging sodium-ion battery technologies.  Current understanding of sodium-storage behavior in hard carbons attributes capacity to filling of graphitic interlayers and pores, and adsorption at defects, although there is still considerable debate regarding the voltages at which these mechanisms occur.  Here, ex situ23Na solid-state NMR and total scattering studies on a systematically tuned series of hard carbons revealed the formation of increasingly metallic sodium clusters in direct correlation to the growing pore size, occurring only in samples which exhibited a low voltage plateau.  Combining exptl. results with DFT calcns., we propose a revised mechanistic model in which sodium ions store first simultaneously and continuously at defects, within interlayers and on pore surfaces.  Once these higher energy binding sites are filled, pore filling occurs during the plateau region, where the densely confined sodium takes on a greater degree of metallicity.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoSrEjXCn1VBrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhXbh1oMLR4UQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsFyktLjJ&amp;md5=a7181f3c171e70a094942fc79cb26548</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.6b03403"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kumar, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Detsi, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abraham, D.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fundamental Mechanisms of Solvent Decomposition Involved in Solid-Electrolyte Interphase Formation in Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kumar, Hemant; Detsi, Eric; Abraham, Daniel P.; Shenoy, Vivek B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
