        </div><div class="casAbstract">Prolonged decompn. of electrolytes forming a thick and unstable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) continues to be a major bottleneck in designing sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  We have carried out quantum chem. simulations to investigate the fundamental mechanisms of redn.-induced decompn. of electrolyte solvents in the vicinity of a sodium ion.  Kinetics and thermodn. of several reaction pathways for one- and two-electron redn. of ethylene carbonate (EC) have been examd.  Our calcns. indicate that the high redn. potential and low barrier for the ring opening of EC is the main cause for the continuous growth of SEI obsd. in SIBs.  The impact of two well-known electrolyte additives, vinyl carbonate (VC) and fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), on SEI compn. was evaluated by studying decompn. pathways of (1) VC and FEC mols. in the bulk EC solvent and (2) an EC mol. in a supermol. cluster comprising an EC and the additive mol.  The additive mols. have significantly low barriers for decompn. and therefore decomp. first.  Addnl., the presence of an additive mol. was also shown to increase the barrier for decompn. of EC.  Another observation suggests that the preferred redn. state of an EC mol. changes when it forms a dimer with additive mols., and these redn. states have different decompn. pathways which leads to formation of different SEI compds.  On the basis of these observations, we predict that not only do the additive mols. protect solvent mols. from reductive decompn. but also they can promote alternate pathways for the decompn., leading to qual. different and potentially stable SEI products.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqcuWCv5dG5bbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljxHBnwMuerMA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvFKnsL%252FL&amp;md5=c0aa1679c7d2b2f9abeff106da5518b4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201706294"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">N-Doping and Defective Nanographitic Domain Coupled Hard Carbon Nanoshells for High Performance Lithium/Sodium Storage</span>. <i>Adv. The exptl. results reveal the staging formation of a single particle within a free standing graphitic electrode.                 10.1021/acs.jpclett.6b01886</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D4291-4296%26journal%3DJ.%2BPhys.%2BChem.%2BLett.%26issue%3D21%26author%3DJ.%2BZou%26author%3DC.%2BSole%26author%3DN.%2BE.%2BDrewett%26author%3DM.%2BVelick%25C3%25BD%26author%3DL.%2BJ.%2BHardwick%26title%3DIn%2BSitu%2BStudy%2Bof%2BLi%2BIntercalation%2Binto%2BHighly%2BCrystalline%2BGraphitic%2BFlakes%2Bof%2BVarying%2BThicknesses%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.jpclett.6b01886&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c12171&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.jpclett.6b01886&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ Study of Li Intercalation into Highly Crystalline Graphitic Flakes of Varying Thicknesses</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zou, Jianli; Sole, Christopher; Drewett, Nicholas E.; Velicky, Matej; Hardwick, Laurence J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4291-4296</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCLCD</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">An in situ Raman spectroelectrochem. study of Li intercalation into graphite flakes with different thicknesses ranging from 1.7 nm (3 graphene layers) to 61 nm (∼178 layers) is presented.  The lithiation behavior of these flakes was compared to com. microcryst. graphite with a typical flake thickness of ∼100 nm.  Li intercalation into the graphitic flakes was obsd. under potential control via in situ optical microscopy and Raman spectroscopy.  As graphite flakes decreased in thickness, a Raman response indicative of increased tensile strain along the graphene sheet was obsd. during the early stages of intercalation.  A progressively neg. wavenumber shift of the interior and bounding modes of the split G band (E2g2(i) and E2g2(b)) is interpreted as a weakening of the C-C bonding.  Raman spectra of Li intercalation into thin graphitic flakes are presented and discussed in the context of implications for Li ion battery applications, given that intercalation induced strain may accelerate carbon neg. electrode aging and reduce long-term cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGothxMb9TonzbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljxHBnwMuerMA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xhs1yks7jN&amp;md5=9b7bb57d409e7a22679ebe85f0d3e214</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1021/nn403885k"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chacón-Torres, J. C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wirtz, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pichler, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Manifestation of Charged and Strained Graphene Layers in the Raman Response of Graphite Intercalation Compounds</span>. <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">10</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">9249</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">9259</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/nn403885k</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D9249-9259%26journal%3DACS%2BNano%26issue%3D10%26author%3DJ.%2BC.%2BChac%25C3%25B3n-Torres%26author%3DL.%2BWirtz%26author%3DT.%2BPichler%26title%3DManifestation%2Bof%2BCharged%2Band%2BStrained%2BGraphene%2BLayers%2Bin%2Bthe%2BRaman%2BResponse%2Bof%2BGraphite%2BIntercalation%2BCompounds%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fnn403885k&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c12171&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fnn403885k&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Manifestation of Charged and Strained Graphene Layers in the Raman Response of Graphite Intercalation Compounds</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chacon-Torres, Julio C.; Wirtz, Ludger; Pichler, Thomas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9249-9259</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors present detailed multi-frequency resonant Raman measurements of potassium graphite intercalation compds. (GICs).  From a well-controlled and consecutive in situ intercalation and high-temp. deintercalation approach, the response of each stage up to stage VI is identified.  The positions of the G and 2D lines as a function of staging depend on the charge transfer from K to the graphite layers and on the lattice expansion.  Ab initio calcns. of the d. and the electronic band structure demonstrate that most (but not all) of the transferred charge remains on the graphene sheets adjacent to the intercalant layers.  This leads to an electronic decoupling of these outer layers from the ones sandwiched between carbon layers and consequently to a decoupling of the corresponding Raman spectra.  Thus, higher stage GICs offer the possibility to measure the vibrations of single, double, and multilayer graphene under conditions of biaxial strain.  This strain can addnl. be correlated to the in-plane lattice consts. of GICs detd. by X-ray diffraction.  The outcome of this study demonstrates that Raman spectroscopy is a very powerful tool to identify local internal strain in pristine and weakly charged single and few-layer graphene and their composites, yielding even abs. lattice consts.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrY4R92Lb-enrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljxHBnwMuerMA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsVehsrrO&amp;md5=b2a5b693d47ff6fc5d65289fd08d4d31</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1039/C6CC06990H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stratford, J. A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grey, C.                 10.1039/C6CC06990H</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D52%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D12430-12433%26journal%3DChem.%2BCommun.%26issue%3D84%26author%3DJ.%2BM.%2BStratford%26author%3DP.%2BK.%2BAllan%26author%3DO.%2BPecher%26author%3DP.%2BA.%2BChater%26author%3DC.%2BP.%2BGrey%26title%3DMechanistic%2BInsights%2Binto%2BSodium%2BStorage%2Bin%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%2BUsing%2BLocal%2BStructure%2BProbes%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC6CC06990H&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c12171&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC6CC06990H&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Mechanistic insights into sodium storage in hard carbon anodes using local structure probes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Stratford, Joshua M.; Allan, Phoebe K.; Pecher, Oliver; Chater, Philip A.; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Communications (Cambridge, United Kingdom)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">52</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12430-12433</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHCOFS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1359-7345</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Operando 23Na solid-state NMR and pair distribution function anal. expts. provide insights into the structure of hard carbon anodes in sodium-ion batteries.  Capacity results from "diamagnetic" sodium ions first adsorbing onto pore surfaces, defects and between expanded layers, before pooling into larger quasi-metallic clusters/expanded carbon sheets at lower voltages.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqZngz65tHR2bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liTOrz6nri6_Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsFGgsL3J&amp;md5=bf8cf33cc822e38565f0995e5fa14fb0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b01969"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Surta, T. L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Mechanistic Insights on Na-Ion Storage in Nongraphitizable Carbon</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">9</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">5888</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5892</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Mechanistic Insights on Na-Ion Storage in Nongraphitizable Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bommier, Clement; Surta, Todd Wesley; Dolgos, Michelle; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5888-5892</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nongraphitizable carbon, also known as hard carbon, is considered one of the most promising anodes for the emerging Na-ion batteries.  The current mechanistic understanding of Na-ion storage in hard carbon is based on the "card-house" model first raised in the early 2000s.  This model describes that Na-ion insertion occurs first through intercalation between graphene sheets in turbostratic nanodomains, followed by Na filling of the pores in the carbon structure.  We tried to test this model by tuning the sizes of turbostratic nanodomains but revealed a correlation between the structural defects and Na-ion storage.  Based on our exptl. data, we propose an alternative perspective for sodiation of hard carbon that consists of Na-ion storage at defect sites, by intercalation and last via pore-filling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqegbEbsDPzrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liTOrz6nri6_Q"> &gt;&gt; Chem. Chem. Phys.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">38</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">15876</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">15887</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c3cp52125g</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Conversion reactions for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Klein, Franziska; Jache, Birte; Bhide, Amrtha; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">38</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15876-15887</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PPCPFQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1463-9076</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Research on Na-ion batteries is focused on finding suitable electrode materials that enable cell reactions of high energy densities combined with low cost.  Naturally, an assessment of potential electrode materials requires a rational comparison with the analog reaction in Li-ion batteries.  The authors discuss the broad range of different conversion reactions for Na-ion batteries based on their basic thermodn. properties and compare them with their Li analogs.  Capacities, voltages, energy densities and vol. expansions are summarized to sketch out the scope for future studies in this research field.  For a given conversion electrode material, replacing Li by Na leads to a const. shift in cell potential ΔEo(Li-Na) depending on the material class.  For chlorides ΔEo(Li-Na) is nearly zero.  The theor. energy densities of conversion reactions of Na with fluorides or chlorides as pos. electrode materials typically reach values between 700 W h kg-1 and 1000 W h kg-1.  Next to the thermodn. assessment, results on several conversion reactions between Cu compds. (CuS, CuO, CuCl, CuCl2) and Na are being discussed.  Reactions with CuS and CuO were chosen because these compds. are frequently studied for conversion reactions with Li.  Chlorides are interesting because of ΔEo(Li-Na) ≈ 0 V As a result of chloride soly. in the electrolyte, the conversion process proceeds at defined potentials under rather small kinetic limitations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvFdD49Ctj47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljC5LtXS4ttLw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsVWrtbrE&amp;md5=7135136abf30b3a83827d65de85c8f45</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201804458"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">An, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanostructured Conversion-Type Negative Electrode Materials for Low-Cost and High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">128</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">133</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201200346</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical performance of porous carbon/tin composite anodes for sodium-ion and lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Yunhua; Zhu, Yujie; Liu, Yihang; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">128-133</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.  Understanding of the reaction mechanism between Sn and Na ions will provide insight towards exploring and designing new alloy-based anode materials for Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqL8pwZXJa53LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljC5LtXS4ttLw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsFKhtr8%253D&amp;md5=7fba4efb710816f1ca877f0494169a46</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201808745"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, H. T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rui, X. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Peering into Alloy Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Current Trends, Challenges, and Opportunities</span>. <i>Adv. Funct. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>29</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">14</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">1808745</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent progress of emerging cathode materials for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Jun; Li, Xiao; Tang, Kaikai; Wang, Dandan; Long, Mengqi; Gao, Hong; Chen, Weihua; Liu, Chuntai; Liu, Hao; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Chemistry Frontiers</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3735-3764</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MCFAC5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2052-1537</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Recently, due to the excessive consumption of fossil energy and the intermittent nature of clean energy resources, electricity storage has attracted great attention from both academia and industry.  Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) with high energy d. have been widely used for portable energy storage systems.  However, limited lithium resources cannot meet the demands for large-scale applications, esp. the grid-scale energy storage.  Owing to the abundant natural resources of sodium and the low price, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have become potential substitutes for LIBs.  Cathode materials are key components that det. the overall performance and entire cost of SIBs.  However, the unstable structure and limited electrochem. performance make them difficult to meet the requirement of practical applications.  This review summarizes the recent development of the cathode materials used for SIBs, and the modified strategies applied to improve their electrochem. performance.  Finally, crit. challenges and perspectives to promote the development of SIBs are also presented.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp8ZUfRDzVosbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljC5LtXS4ttLw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXmvVymtrw%253D&amp;md5=6a905d575e4f69f242f907539d23eada</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201400794"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.                 10.1002/adma.201400794</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sn4+xP3 amorphous Sn-P composites as anodes for sodium-ion batteries with low cost, high capacity, long Life, and superior rate capability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Weijie; Chou, Shu-Lei; Wang, Jia-Zhao; Kim, Jung Ho; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">24</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn4+xP3@(Sn-P) composite was prepd. in large quantities by direct low-speed ball milling of the P and Snpowders and studied its electrochem. performance as anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  The results demonstrated that the Sn4+xP3·(Sn-P) electrode using CMC binder delivered an initial discharge capacity of 1030 mA h g-1 and a reversible charge capacity of 892 mA h g-1, at the c.d. of 100 mA g-1.  The addn. of 5% FEC to the electrolyte can improve the cycling stability of the Sn4+xP3 electrode exhibited superior high rate capability, with a stable capacity of 165 mA h g-1 at the 10 C rate (5000 mA g-1).  All the resultes indicate that Sn4+xP3·(Sn-P) would be a promising anode material candidate for sodium-ion batteries with low cost, long cycling stability, and relatively good rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqoO0b7cRbjN7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljC5LtXS4ttLw"> &gt;&gt;                         rightTab-cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard Carbon Anodes: Fundamental Understanding and Commercial Perspectives for Na-Ion Batteries beyond Li-Ion and K-Ion Counterparts</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Ling-Fei; Hu, Zhe; Lai, Wei-Hong; Tao, Ying; Peng, Jian; Miao, Zong-Cheng; Wang, Yun-Xiao; Chou, Shu-Lei; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Hard carbon (HC) is recognized as a promising anode material with outstanding electrochem. performance for alkali metal-ion batteries including lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), as well as their analogs sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs).  Herein, a comprehensive review of the recent research is presented to interpret the challenges and opportunities for the applications of HC anodes.  The ion storage mechanisms, materials design, and electrolyte optimizations for alkali metal-ion batteries are illustrated in-depth.  HC is particularly promising as an anode material for SIBs.  The solid-electrolyte interphase, initial Coulombic efficiency, safety concerns, and all-climate performances, which are vital for practical applications, are comprehensively discussed.  Furthermore, com. prototypes of SIBs based on HC anodes are extensively elaborated.  The remaining challenges and research perspectives are provided, aiming to shed light on future research and early commercialization of HC-based SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrgUes-Iv_xOrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ligP_oT1HrpSQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXisVagtLrM&amp;md5=dc06d1bd2c8724df623e45590cac38e2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703238"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.                         rightTab-cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Raman microspectroscopy of soot and related carbonaceous materials. Spectral analysis and structural information</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sadezky, A.; Muckenhuber, H.; Grothe, H.; Niessner, R.; Poeschl, U.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2005</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">43</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Exptl. conditions and math. fitting procedures for the collection and anal. of Raman spectra of soot and related carbonaceous materials were investigated and optimized with a Raman microscope system operated at 3 different laser excitation wavelengths (514, 633, and 780 nm).  Several band combinations for spectral anal. were tested, and a combination of 4 Lorentzian-shaped bands (G, D1, D2, D4) at about 1580, 1350, 1620, and 1200 cm-1, resp., with a Gaussian-shaped band (D3) at ∼1500 cm-1 was best suited for the 1st-order spectra.  The 2nd-order spectra were best fitted with Lorentzian-shaped bands at about 2450, 2700, 2900, and 3100 cm-1.  Spectral parameters (band positions, full widths at half max., and intensity ratios) are reported for several types of industrial C black (Degussa Printex, Cabot Monarch), diesel soot (particulate matter from modern heavy duty vehicle and passenger car engine exhaust, NIST SRM1650), spark-discharge soot (Palas GfG100), and graphite.  Several parameters, in particular the width of the D1 band at ∼1350 cm-1, provide structural information and allow to discriminate the sample materials, but the characterization and distinction of different types of soot is limited by the exptl. reproducibility of the spectra and the statistical uncertainties of curve fitting.  The results are discussed and compared with x-ray diffraction measurements and earlier Raman spectroscopic studies of comparable materials, where different measurement and fitting procedures was applied.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqI47SxmqUfxrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ligP_oT1HrpSQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2MXltFGrtLc%253D&amp;md5=045e6d9ee2eb3d84bd001fca25bc7f62</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref17" class="rightTabRefNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit17" data-doi="10.3390/c1010077"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bokobza, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bruneel, J.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Couzi, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Raman Spectra of Carbon-Based Materials (from Graphite to Carbon Black) and of Some Silicone Composites</span>. <i>C J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Predicted soot concns. from numerical simulations of N2-dild., ethylene/air flames were compared with laser-induced incandescence and Raman spectra obsd. from samples thermophoretically extd. using a rapid insertion technique.  In some flame regions, Raman spectra were obscured by intense radiation which appeared to peak in the near IR spectral region.  There was good agreement between spatial profiles of this ex-situ, laser-induced incandescence (ES-LII) and traditional in-situ, laser-induced incandescence (IS-LII).  Raman signatures were obsd. from low in the flame and extended into the upper flame regions. Several routines were examd. to de-convolve the data, including 3- and 5-band models and a 2-band Breit-Wigner-Fano model.  Since Raman signals were obsd. at heights below those where in-situ LII was obsd., it was postulated that these signals may be attributable to smaller particles.  Results suggested the obsd.                         rightTab-cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, Yang; He, Kai; Zhu, Yujie; Han, Fudong; Xu, Yunhua; Matsuda, Isamu; Ishii, Yoshitaka; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrFEZ8wJYk1irVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ligP_oT1HrpSQ"> &gt;&gt;                         rightTab-cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode: Tunable Local Structures and Sodium Storage Mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Yu; Sun, Shixiong; Ou, Mingyang; Liu, Yi; Fan, Chenyang; Sun, Xueping; Peng, Jian; Li, Yuyu; Qiu, Yuegang; Wei, Peng; Deng, Zhi; Xu, Yue; Han, Jiantao; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon (HC) is one of the most promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its suitable potential and high reversible capacity.  At the same time, the correlation between carbon local structure and sodium-ion storage behavior is not clearly understood.  In this paper, the two series of HC materials with perfect spherical morphol. and tailored microstructures were designed and successfully produced using resorcinol formaldehyde (RF) resin as precursor.  Via hydrothermal self-assembly and controlled pyrolysis, RF is a flexible precursor for high-purity carbon with a wide range of local-structure variation.  Using these processes, one series of five representative RF-based HC nanospheres with varying degrees of graphitization were obtained from an RF precursor at different carbonization temps.  The other series of HC materials with various microscopic carbon layer lengths and shapes was achieved by carbonizing five RF precursors with different crosslinking degrees at a single carbonization condition (1300 °C and 2 h).  On the basis of the microstructures, unique electrochem. characteristics, and at. pair distribution function (PDF) analyses, we proposed a new model of "three-phase" structural for HC materials and found triregion Na-ion storage behavior: chemi-/physisorption, intercalation between carbon layers, and pore-filling, derived from the HC phases, resp.         </div><div class="casAbstract">While the technol. importance of carbon-based anodes for sodium-ion batteries is undebated, the underlying mechanism for sodium insertion and storage is still strongly disputed.  Here, a joint exptl. and theor. study is presented that allows to provide detailed insights into the process of Na insertion in nongraphitizable (hard) carbon.  For this purpose, the data from in situ Raman scattering of Na insertion in hard carbon are combined with d. functional theory-based lattice dynamics and band structure calcns. for Na insertion in graphitic model structures used for a local description of graphitic domains in hard carbon.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Although many studies have demonstrated the excellent potential of hard carbon as an anode in sodium ion batteries, the contribution of its active sites to the capacities of the sloping and plateau voltage regions is not yet clear.  Herein, systematical investigation of the relationship between the active sites and sodium ion (Na+) storage in the sloping and plateau voltage regions was presented.  In light of the physicochem. properties of the lignin-derived hard carbon (graphitization degree, interlayer spacing, micropore size distribution, and sp. surface area), the results of Na+ ion diffusivity, and the change in these properties during Na+ ion insertion/extn. (as characterized by ex situ techniques), new mechanistic insights into Na+ ion storage were proposed.  At the beginning of the sodiation process, Na+ ions were adsorbed on defect/edge sites; then partial micropore filling occurred in the sloping region above 0.1 V.  In the plateau region below 0.1 V, Na+ ions were intercalated in the graphitic layers, and further adsorption in the micropores occurred near the cutoff potential.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Operando microbeam Raman spectroscopy is used to map the changes in hard carbon during sodiation and desodiation in unprecedented detail, elucidating several important and unresolved aspects of the sodiation mechanism.  On sodiation a substantial, reversible decrease in G-peak energy is obsd., which corresponds directly to the sloping part of the voltage profile and we argue can only be due to steady intercalation of sodium between the turbostratic layers of the hard carbon.  The corresponding reversibility of the D-peak energy change is consistent with intercalation rather than representing a permanent increase in disorder.  No change in energy of the graphitic phonons occurs over the low-voltage plateau, indicating that intercalation sats. before sodium clusters form in micropores in this region.  At the start of the initial sodiation there is no change in G- and D-peak energy as the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) forms.  After SEI formation, the background slope of the spectra increases irreversibly due to fluorescence.  The importance of in situ/operando expts. over ex situ studies is demonstrated; washing the samples or air exposure causes the G- and D-peaks to revert back to their original states because of SEI removal and sodium deintercalation and confirms no permanent damage to the carbon structure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoEDvtHvHdASrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7RhDk6VMPEQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsFGks77E&amp;md5=c8fdf72638e2a4b8bfa2b6c3e6c0ce2c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref27" class="rightTabRefNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit27" data-doi="10.1039/D0EE01363C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Au, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alptekin, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jensen, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons have shown considerable promise as anodes for emerging sodium-ion battery technologies.  Current understanding of sodium-storage behavior in hard carbons attributes capacity to filling of graphitic interlayers and pores, and adsorption at defects, although there is still considerable debate regarding the voltages at which these mechanisms occur.  Here, ex situ23Na solid-state NMR and total scattering studies on a systematically tuned series of hard carbons revealed the formation of increasingly metallic sodium clusters in direct correlation to the growing pore size, occurring only in samples which exhibited a low voltage plateau.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Prolonged decompn. of electrolytes forming a thick and unstable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) continues to be a major bottleneck in designing sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).     <span class="NLM_cas:pages">223-237</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">FDISE6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1359-6640</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The first and second order Raman spectra of graphite during the first lithiation and delithiation have been investigated in a typical lithium-ion battery electrolyte.  In situ, real-time Raman measurements under potential control enable the probing of the graphitic neg. electrode surface region during ion insertion and extn.  The exptl. results reveal the staging formation of a single particle within a free standing graphitic electrode.  In particular, the in situ behavior of the double resonance 2D band during the lithiation and delithiation of graphitic carbon has not been previously reported.  The 2D band was obsd. to shift from 2681 to 2611 cm-1 and the band shape transformed into a single Lorentzian from 0.24 to 0.15 V vs.  Li/Li+, providing further information on the electronic structure and C-C bonding of stage 3 and 4 graphite intercalation compds.  The behavior of the 2D band is in keeping with the Daumas-He´rold model of electrochem. derived intercalation, where the graphene layers are flexible and deform around domains of intercalating lithium ions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpOczA4KSDQ07Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgOI7E19pEw5g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXht1KktbzE&amp;md5=8116c370e8dfe22c02fc8f878dc4425b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1149/1.2043869"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Inaba, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoshida, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogumi, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abe, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mizutani, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Asano, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In Situ Raman Study on Electrochemical Li Intercalation into Graphite</span>. <i>J.                 10.1149/1.2043869</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ Raman study on electrochemical Li intercalation into graphite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Inaba, Minoru; Yoshida, Hiroyuki; Ogumi, Zempachi; Abe, Takeshi; Mizutani, Yasuo; Asano, Mitsuru</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1995</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">142</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">20-6</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. lithium intercalation into highly oriented pyrolytic graphite and natural graphite powder was studied using in situ Raman spectroscopy.  Three plateaus were obsd. on the charging curve for both samples.  From the Raman spectral changes the 1st plateau was assigned to a phase transition from dil. stage 1 to stage 4, the 2nd from a stage 2 phase to another stage 2 phase, and the 3rd from stage 2 to stage 1, which are in good agreement with Dahn's results by in situ x-ray diffraction.  The spectral changes assocd. with the phase transitions occurred reversibly during a charge and discharge cycle.  It was shown from the Raman spectral changes of the HOPG electrode that the electrode potential during the electrochem. intercalation is detd. by the surface stage of graphite intercalation compds.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpX0prhgle-7bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgOI7E19pEw5g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK2MXivFeitLY%253D&amp;md5=423aa28e3acef53372ec795b389a96a3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref32" class="rightTabRefNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit32" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpclett.6b01886"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sole, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Drewett, N.                         rightTab-cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ Study of Li Intercalation into Highly Crystalline Graphitic Flakes of Varying Thicknesses</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zou, Jianli; Sole, Christopher; Drewett, Nicholas E.; Velicky, Matej; Hardwick, Laurence J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry Letters</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">An in situ Raman spectroelectrochem. study of Li intercalation into graphite flakes with different thicknesses ranging from 1.7 nm (3 graphene layers) to 61 nm (∼178 layers) is presented.  The lithiation behavior of these flakes was compared to com. microcryst. graphite with a typical flake thickness of ∼100 nm.  Li intercalation into the graphitic flakes was obsd. under potential control via in situ optical microscopy and Raman spectroscopy.  As graphite flakes decreased in thickness, a Raman response indicative of increased tensile strain along the graphene sheet was obsd. during the early stages of intercalation.  A progressively neg. wavenumber shift of the interior and bounding modes of the split G band (E2g2(i) and E2g2(b)) is interpreted as a weakening of the C-C bonding.  Raman spectra of Li intercalation into thin graphitic flakes are presented and discussed in the context of implications for Li ion battery applications, given that intercalation induced strain may accelerate carbon neg. electrode aging and reduce long-term cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGothxMb9TonzbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgOI7E19pEw5g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xhs1yks7jN&amp;md5=9b7bb57d409e7a22679ebe85f0d3e214</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1021/nn403885k"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chacón-Torres, J.                 10.1021/nn403885k</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Manifestation of Charged and Strained Graphene Layers in the Raman Response of Graphite Intercalation Compounds</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chacon-Torres, Julio C.; Wirtz, Ludger; Pichler, Thomas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9249-9259</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors present detailed multi-frequency resonant Raman measurements of potassium graphite intercalation compds. (GICs).  From a well-controlled and consecutive in situ intercalation and high-temp. deintercalation approach, the response of each stage up to stage VI is identified.  The positions of the G and 2D lines as a function of staging depend on the charge transfer from K to the graphite layers and on the lattice expansion.  Ab initio calcns. of the d. and the electronic band structure demonstrate that most (but not all) of the transferred charge remains on the graphene sheets adjacent to the intercalant layers.  This leads to an electronic decoupling of these outer layers from the ones sandwiched between carbon layers and consequently to a decoupling of the corresponding Raman spectra.  Thus, higher stage GICs offer the possibility to measure the vibrations of single, double, and multilayer graphene under conditions of biaxial strain.  This strain can addnl. be correlated to the in-plane lattice consts. of GICs detd. by X-ray diffraction.  The outcome of this study demonstrates that Raman spectroscopy is a very powerful tool to identify local internal strain in pristine and weakly charged single and few-layer graphene and their composites, yielding even abs. lattice consts.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrY4R92Lb-enrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgOI7E19pEw5g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsVehsrrO&amp;md5=b2a5b693d47ff6fc5d65289fd08d4d31</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1039/C6CC06990H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stratford, J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Operando 23Na solid-state NMR and pair distribution function anal. expts. provide insights into the structure of hard carbon anodes in sodium-ion batteries. L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Mechanistic Insights on Na-Ion Storage in Nongraphitizable Carbon</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">9</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">5888</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5892</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b01969</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Mechanistic Insights on Na-Ion Storage in Nongraphitizable Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bommier, Clement; Surta, Todd Wesley; Dolgos, Michelle; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5888-5892</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nongraphitizable carbon, also known as hard carbon, is considered one of the most promising anodes for the emerging Na-ion batteries.  The current mechanistic understanding of Na-ion storage in hard carbon is based on the "card-house" model first raised in the early 2000s.  This model describes that Na-ion insertion occurs first through intercalation between graphene sheets in turbostratic nanodomains, followed by Na filling of the pores in the carbon structure.  We tried to test this model by tuning the sizes of turbostratic nanodomains but revealed a correlation between the structural defects and Na-ion storage.  Based on our exptl. data, we propose an alternative perspective for sodiation of hard carbon that consists of Na-ion storage at defect sites, by intercalation and last via pore-filling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqegbEbsDPzrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgOI7E19pEw5g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1Oqsb3M&amp;md5=a2144d9fa8c9f19a479ebec0ec74f1eb</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i15">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c12171?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c12171</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Digital picture of the material sandwiched between graphite boards, and SEM image of the carbon paper; digital photograph of large size carbon paper; comparison of XRD, Raman, and infrared spectra of soft carbon; rate performance of HC-P-1200; Nyquist plots of HC-P-1200 and HC-W-1200; full information on operando Raman spectra of the charge–discharge process; periodic variation of the intensity of the charge–discharge for the first cycle, and slope of the second cycle at 1000 and 2000 cm<sup>–1</sup> Raman shift (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12171/suppl_file/am1c12171_si_001.pdf">am1c12171_si_001.pdf (1.18 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {

	    }

	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){

<script nonce="90f6e41b4cb2775c-LHR">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>


                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90f6e41b4cb2775c-LHR">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
